</div></th><td>2 December 1971</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><div style="text-indent:-0.9em;margin-left:1.2em;font-weight:normal;">•&#160;<a style="text-align:center"><div style="text-align:left;margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><div style="background:#ddf;padding:0.2em;font-size:135%;text-align:center;"><b><a href="/wiki/Portal:United_Arab_Emirates" title="Portal:United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates portal</a></b></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>United Arab Emirates</b> (<b>UAE</b>; <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">دولة الإمارات العربية المتحدة</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">Dawlat al-ʾImārāt al-ʿArabīyyah al-Muttaḥidah</i></i> literally "'State' of the United Arab Emirates"), sometimes simply called the <b>Emirates</b> (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl">الإمارات</span>&#8206; <i><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language romanization">al-ʾImārāt</i></i>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> at the southeast end of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, bordering <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a> to the east and <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> to the south and west, as well as sharing maritime borders with <a href="/wiki/Qatar" title="Qatar">Qatar</a> to the west and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> to the north. The <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign</a> <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_monarchy" title="Constitutional monarchy">constitutional monarchy</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Federation" title="Federation">federation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emirates_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Emirates of the United Arab Emirates">seven emirates</a> consisting of <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Abu_Dhabi" title="Emirate of Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a> (which serves as the capital), <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Ajman" title="Emirate of Ajman">Ajman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Dubai" title="Emirate of Dubai">Dubai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fujairah" class="mw-redirect" title="Fujairah">Fujairah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ras_al-Khaimah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ras al-Khaimah">Ras Al Khaimah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sharjah" title="Emirate of Sharjah">Sharjah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umm_al-Quwain" class="mw-redirect" title="Umm al-Quwain">Umm Al Quwain</a>. Their boundaries are complex, with numerous <a href="/wiki/Enclaves" class="mw-redirect" title="Enclaves">enclaves</a> within the various emirates.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>  Each emirate is governed by a ruler; together, they jointly form the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Supreme_Council" title="Federal Supreme Council">Federal Supreme Council</a>. One of the rulers serves as the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="President of the United Arab Emirates">President of the United Arab Emirates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, the UAE's population was 9.2 million, of which 1.4 million are <a href="/wiki/Emiratis" title="Emiratis">Emirati citizens</a> and 7.8 million are <a href="/wiki/Expatriates_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Expatriates in the United Arab Emirates">expatriates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-natur_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natur-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Human occupation of the present UAE has been traced back to the emergence of anatomically modern humans from Africa some 125,000 BCE through finds at the Faya-1 site in <a href="/wiki/Mleiha_Archaeological_Centre" title="Mleiha Archaeological Centre">Mleiha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Sharjah" title="Emirate of Sharjah">Sharjah</a>. Burial sites dating back to the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Neolithic Age">Neolithic Age</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> include the oldest known such inland site at <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Al-Buhais" class="mw-redirect" title="Jebel Al-Buhais">Jebel Buhais</a>. Known as <a href="/wiki/Magan_(civilization)" title="Magan (civilization)">Magan</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerians</a>, the area was home to a prosperous Bronze Age trading culture during the <a href="/wiki/Umm_al-Nar_culture" title="Umm al-Nar culture">Umm Al Nar period</a>, which traded between the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dilmun" title="Dilmun">Bahrain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Suq_culture" title="Wadi Suq culture">Wadi Suq period</a> and three <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Iron Age in the United Arab Emirates">Iron Ages</a> saw the emergence of nomadism as well as the development of water management and irrigation systems supporting human settlement in both the coast and interior. The Islamic age of the UAE dates back to the expulsion of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanians</a> and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dibba" title="Battle of Dibba">Battle of Dibba</a>. The UAE's long history of trade led to the emergence of <a href="/wiki/Julfar" class="mw-redirect" title="Julfar">Julfar</a>, in the present-day emirate of Ras Al Khaimah, as a major regional trading and maritime hub in the area. The maritime dominance of the Persian Gulf by Emirati traders led to conflicts with European powers, including the Portuguese and British.
</p><p>Following decades of maritime conflict, the coastal emirates became known as the <a href="/wiki/Trucial_States" title="Trucial States">Trucial States</a> with the signing of a Perpetual Treaty of Maritime Peace with the British in 1819 (ratified in 1853 and again in 1892), which established the Trucial States as a British <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">Protectorate</a>. This arrangement ended with independence and the establishment of the United Arab Emirates on 2 December 1971, immediately following the British withdrawal from its treaty obligations. Six emirates joined the UAE in 1971, the seventh, Ras Al Khaimah, joined the federation on 10 February 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Official_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Official religion">official religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> is the official language of the UAE. The <a href="/wiki/Oil_reserves_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Oil reserves in the United Arab Emirates">UAE's oil reserves</a> are the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_proven_oil_reserves" title="List of countries by proven oil reserves">seventh-largest in the world</a> while its natural gas reserves are the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_proven_reserves" title="List of countries by natural gas proven reserves">seventeenth-largest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Zayed" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikh Zayed">Sheikh Zayed</a>, ruler of Abu Dhabi and the first President of the UAE, oversaw the development of the Emirates and steered oil revenues into healthcare, education and infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The UAE's economy is the most diversified in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a>, while its most populous city of <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a> is an important <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">global city</a> and international aviation and maritime trade hub.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Consequently, the country is much less reliant on oil and gas than in previous years and is economically focusing on tourism and business. The UAE government does not levy income tax although there is a system of corporate tax in place and <a href="/wiki/Value-added_tax" title="Value-added tax">value added tax</a> was established in 2018 at 5%.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The UAE's rising international profile has led to it being recognised as a regional and a <a href="/wiki/Middle_power" title="Middle power">middle power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> It is a member of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>, <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Cooperation_Council" title="Gulf Cooperation Council">Gulf Cooperation Council</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="History of the United Arab Emirates">History of the United Arab Emirates</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Strait_of_hormuz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Strait_of_hormuz.jpg/220px-Strait_of_hormuz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="319" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Strait_of_hormuz.jpg/330px-Strait_of_hormuz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Strait_of_hormuz.jpg/440px-Strait_of_hormuz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="938" data-file-height="1360" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Strait_of_hormuz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1892 map of Arabia denoting the <i>Pirate Coast</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg/220px-Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg/330px-Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg/440px-Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3024" data-file-height="3024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iron_Age_Pot_From_Bidaa_Bint_Saud.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pot discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> building of <a href="/wiki/Bidaa_Bint_Saud" title="Bidaa Bint Saud">Bidaa Bint Saud</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Ain" title="Al Ain">Al Ain</a> on display at the <a href="/wiki/Al_Ain_National_Museum" title="Al Ain National Museum">Al Ain National Museum</a>. It is thought to be an incense burner.</div></div></div>
<p>The land of the Emirates has been occupied for thousands of years. Stone tools recovered from Jebel Faya in the emirate of Sharjah reveal a settlement of people from <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> some 127,000 years ago and a stone tool used for butchering animals discovered at Jebel Barakah on the Arabian coast suggests an even older habitation from 130,000 years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> There is no proof of contact with the outside world at that stage, although in time lively trading links developed with civilisations in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, Iran and the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Harappan culture</a> of the Indus Valley. This contact persisted and became wide-ranging, probably motivated by the trade in copper from the <a href="/wiki/Al_Hajar_Mountains" title="Al Hajar Mountains">Hajar Mountains</a>, which commenced around 3,000 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Sumerian sources talk of the UAE as home to the 'Makkan' or <a href="/wiki/Magan_(civilization)" title="Magan (civilization)">Magan</a> people.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are six major periods of human settlement with distinctive behaviours in the pre-Islamic UAE, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Hafit_period" title="Hafit period">Hafit period</a> from 3,200-2,600 BCE; the <a href="/wiki/Umm_al-Nar_culture" title="Umm al-Nar culture">Umm Al Nar culture</a> spanned from 2,600-2,000 BCE, the <a href="/wiki/Wadi_Suq_culture" title="Wadi Suq culture">Wadi Suq people</a> dominated from 2,000–1,300 BCE. From 1,200 BC to the advent of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in Eastern Arabia, through three distinctive <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age_in_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="Iron Age in the United Arab Emirates">Iron Ages</a> (Iron Age 1, 1,200–1,000 BC; Iron Age 2, 1,000–600 BC and Iron Age 3 600–300 BC) and the Mleiha period (300 BC onward), the area was variously occupied by <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> and other forces and saw the construction of fortified settlements and extensive husbandry thanks to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Qanat" title="Qanat">falaj</a> irrigation system.
</p><p>In ancient times, Al Hasa (today's Eastern Province of Saudi Arabia) was part of Al Bahreyn and adjoined Greater Oman (today's UAE and <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>). From the second century AD, there was a movement of tribes from Al Bahreyn towards the lower Gulf, together with a migration among the Azdite Qahtani (or Yamani) and Quda'ah tribal groups from south-west Arabia towards central Oman.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The spread of Islam to the North Eastern tip of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> is thought to have followed directly from a letter sent by the Islamic prophet, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, to the rulers of Oman in 630 AD, nine years after the <a href="/wiki/Hegira" title="Hegira">hijrah</a>. This led to a group of rulers travelling to <a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">Medina</a>, converting to Islam and subsequently driving a successful uprising against the unpopular Sasanids, who dominated the Northern coasts at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Following the death of Muhammad, the new Islamic communities south of the Persian Gulf threatened to disintegrate, with insurrections against the Muslim leaders. The <a href="/wiki/Abu_Bakr" title="Abu Bakr">Caliph Abu Bakr</a> sent an army from the capital Medina which completed its reconquest of the territory (<a href="/wiki/Ridda_wars" title="Ridda wars">the Ridda Wars</a>) with the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dibba" title="Battle of Dibba">Battle of Dibba</a> in which 10,000 lives are thought to have been lost.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> This assured the integrity of the <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> and the unification of the Arabian Peninsula under the newly emerging <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>.
</p><p>In 637, Julfar (in the area of today's Ras Al Khaimah) was an important port that was used as a staging post for the Islamic invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-AbedHellyer2001_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AbedHellyer2001-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The area of the Al Ain/Buraimi Oasis was known as Tu'am and was an important trading post for camel routes between the coast and the Arabian interior.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> site in the UAE was first discovered in the 1990s, an extensive monastic complex on what is now known as <a href="/wiki/Sir_Bani_Yas" title="Sir Bani Yas">Sir Bani Yas Island</a> and which dates back to the 7th century. Thought to be Nestorian and built in 600 AD, the church appears to have been abandoned peacefully in 750 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> It forms a rare physical link to a legacy of Christianity which is thought to have spread across the peninsula from 50 to 350 AD following trade routes. Certainly, by the 5th century, Oman had a bishop named John – the last bishop of Oman being Etienne, in 676 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_and_Portuguese_era">Ottoman and Portuguese era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ottoman and Portuguese era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Piracy_in_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Piracy in the Persian Gulf">Piracy in the Persian Gulf</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_%28cropped%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1214" data-file-height="844" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Travels_in_Assyria_Ras_Al_Khaimah_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>‘Ras-el-Khyma, the chief port of the Wahabee pirates’ (1829) by <a href="/wiki/James_Silk_Buckingham" title="James Silk Buckingham">James Silk Buckingham</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The harsh desert environment led to the emergence of the "versatile tribesman", nomadic groups who subsisted due to a variety of economic activities, including animal husbandry, agriculture and hunting. The seasonal movements of these groups led to not only frequent clashes between groups but also the establishment of seasonal and semi-seasonal settlements and centres. These formed tribal groupings whose names are still carried by modern Emiratis, including the <a href="/wiki/Bani_Yas" title="Bani Yas">Bani Yas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al_Nahyan_family" title="Al Nahyan family">Al Bu Falah</a> of Abu Dhabi, Al Ain, Liwa and the west coast, the <a href="/wiki/Dhawahir" title="Dhawahir">Dhawahir</a>, Awamir, <a href="/wiki/Al-Ali_(tribe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ali (tribe)">Al Ali</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manasir_(tribe)" title="Manasir (tribe)">Manasir</a> of the interior, the <a href="/wiki/Sharqiyin" title="Sharqiyin">Sharqiyin</a> of the east coast and the <a href="/wiki/Al-Qasimi" title="Al-Qasimi">Qawasim</a> to the North.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the expansion of European colonialism, Portuguese, English and Dutch forces appeared in the Persian Gulf region. By the 18th century, the Bani Yas confederation was the dominant force in most of the area now known as Abu Dhabi,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> while the Northern <a href="/wiki/Al_Qawasim" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Qawasim">Al Qawasim</a> (Al Qasimi) dominated maritime commerce. The Portuguese maintained an influence over the coastal settlements, building <a href="/wiki/Fort" class="mw-redirect" title="Fort">forts</a> in the wake of the bloody 16th-century conquests of coastal communities by <a href="/wiki/Afonso_de_Albuquerque" title="Afonso de Albuquerque">Albuquerque</a> and the Portuguese commanders who followed him – particularly on the east coast at <a href="/wiki/Muscat,_Oman" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscat, Oman">Muscat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sohar" title="Sohar">Sohar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khor_Fakkan" title="Khor Fakkan">Khor Fakkan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The southern coast of the Persian Gulf was known to the British as the "<a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="History of the United Arab Emirates">Pirate Coast</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> as boats of the Al Qawasim federation harassed British-flagged shipping from the 17th century into the 19th.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The charge of piracy is disputed by modern Emirati historians, including the current Ruler of Sharjah, <a href="/wiki/Sultan_bin_Muhammad_Al-Qasimi" title="Sultan bin Muhammad Al-Qasimi">Sheikh Sultan Al Qasim</a>i, in his 1986 book <i>The Myth of Arab Piracy in the Gulf</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack,_1809_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack%2C_1809_01.jpg/220px-Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack%2C_1809_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack%2C_1809_01.jpg/330px-Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack%2C_1809_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack%2C_1809_01.jpg/440px-Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack%2C_1809_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2238" data-file-height="1289" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ras_Al_Khaimah_under_attack,_1809_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A painting depicting the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf_campaign_of_1809" title="Persian Gulf campaign of 1809">British Expeditionary Force in 1809</a> sacking the coastal town and port of <a href="/wiki/Ras_Al_Khaimah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ras Al Khaimah">Ras Al Khaimah</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>British bloody expeditions to protect the Indian trade led to campaigns against Ras Al Khaimah and other harbours along the coast, including the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf_campaign_of_1809" title="Persian Gulf campaign of 1809">Persian Gulf Campaign of 1809</a> and the more successful <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf_campaign_of_1819" title="Persian Gulf campaign of 1819">campaign of 1819</a>. The following year, Britain and a number of local rulers signed a <a href="/wiki/General_Maritime_Treaty_of_1820" title="General Maritime Treaty of 1820">maritime truce</a>, giving rise to the term <a href="/wiki/Trucial_States" title="Trucial States">Trucial States</a>, which came to define the status of the coastal emirates. A further treaty was signed in 1843 and, in 1853 the Perpetual Treaty of Maritime Truce was agreed. To this was added the 'Exclusive Agreements', signed in 1892, which made the <a href="/wiki/Trucial_States" title="Trucial States">Trucial States</a> a British protectorate.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Under the 1892 treaty, the trucial sheikhs agreed not to dispose of any territory except to the British and not to enter into relationships with any foreign government other than the British without their consent. In return, the British promised to protect the Trucial Coast from all aggression by sea and to help in case of land attack. The Exclusive Agreement was signed by the Rulers of Abu Dhabi, Dubai, Sharjah, Ajman, Ras Al Khaimah and <a href="/wiki/Umm_Al_Quwain" title="Umm Al Quwain">Umm Al Quwain</a> between 6 and 8 March 1892. It was subsequently ratified by the <a href="/wiki/Viceroy_of_India" class="mw-redirect" title="Viceroy of India">Viceroy of India</a> and the British Government in London.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> British maritime policing meant that pearling fleets could operate in relative security. However, the British prohibition of the <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a> meant an important source of income was lost to some sheikhs and merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1869, the Qubaisat tribe settled at <a href="/wiki/Khawr_al_Udayd" title="Khawr al Udayd">Khawr al Udayd</a> and tried to enlist the support of the Ottomans, whose flag was occasionally seen flying there. Khawr al Udayd was claimed by Abu Dhabi at that time, a claim supported by the British. In 1906, the British Political Resident, <a href="/wiki/Percy_Cox" title="Percy Cox">Percy Cox</a>, confirmed in writing to the ruler of Abu Dhabi, <a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Khalifa_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Khalifa Al Nahyan">Zayed bin Khalifa Al Nahyan</a> ('Zayed the Great') that Khawr al Udayd belonged to his sheikhdom.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_era_and_discovery_of_oil">British era and discovery of oil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: British era and discovery of oil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During the 19th and early 20th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_hunting" title="Pearl hunting">pearling industry</a> thrived, providing both income and employment to the people of the Persian Gulf. The <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First World War</a> had a severe impact on the industry, but it was the <a href="/wiki/Depression_(economics)" title="Depression (economics)">economic depression</a> of the late 1920s and early 1930s, coupled with the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Cultured_pearl" title="Cultured pearl">cultured pearl</a>, that wiped out the trade. The remnants of the trade eventually faded away shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>, when the newly-independent <a href="/wiki/Government_of_India" title="Government of India">Government of India</a> imposed heavy taxation on pearls imported from the <a href="/wiki/Arab_states_of_the_Persian_Gulf" title="Arab states of the Persian Gulf">Arab states of the Persian Gulf</a>. The decline of pearling resulted in extreme economic hardship in the Trucial States.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1922, the British government secured undertakings from the rulers of the Trucial States not to sign concessions with foreign companies without their consent. Aware of the potential for the development of natural resources such as oil, following finds in Persia (from 1908) and Mesopotamia (from 1927), a British-led oil company, the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_Petroleum_Company" title="Iraq Petroleum Company">Iraq Petroleum Company</a> (IPC), showed an interest in the region. The <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Persian_Oil_Company" title="Anglo-Persian Oil Company">Anglo-Persian Oil Company</a> (APOC, later to become British Petroleum, or BP) had a 23.75% share in IPC. From 1935, onshore concessions to explore for oil were granted by local rulers, with APOC signing the first one on behalf of Petroleum Concessions Ltd (PCL), an associate company of IPC.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> APOC was prevented from developing the region alone because of the restrictions of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Line_Agreement" title="Red Line Agreement">Red Line Agreement</a>, which required it to operate through IPC. A number of options between PCL and the trucial rulers were signed, providing useful revenue for communities experiencing poverty following the collapse of the pearl trade. However, the wealth of oil which the rulers could see from the revenues accruing to surrounding countries such as Iran, Bahrain, Kuwait, Qatar and Saudi Arabia remained elusive. The first bore holes in Abu Dhabi were drilled by IPC's operating company, Petroleum Development (Trucial Coast) Ltd (PDTC) at Ras Sadr in 1950, with a 13,000-foot-deep (4,000-metre) bore hole taking a year to drill and turning out dry, at the tremendous cost at the time of £1 million.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG/220px-Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG/330px-Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG/440px-Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1591" data-file-height="1328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mid-20th_century_Dubai.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dubai in 1950; the area in this photo shows <a href="/wiki/Bur_Dubai" title="Bur Dubai">Bur Dubai</a> in the foreground (centered on Al-Fahidi Fort); <a href="/wiki/Deira,_Dubai" title="Deira, Dubai">Deira</a> in middle-right on the other side of the creek; and <a href="/wiki/Al_Shindagha" title="Al Shindagha">Al Shindagha</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Al_Ras,_Dubai" title="Al Ras, Dubai">Al Ras</a> (right) in the background across the creek again from Deira</div></div></div>
<p>The British set up a development office that helped in some small developments in the emirates. The seven <a href="/wiki/Sheikhs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sheikhs">sheikhs</a> of the emirates then decided to form a council to coordinate matters between them and took over the development office. In 1952, they formed the Trucial States Council,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> and appointed <a href="/wiki/Adi_Bitar" title="Adi Bitar">Adi Bitar</a>, Dubai's <a href="/wiki/Rashid_bin_Saeed_Al_Maktoum" title="Rashid bin Saeed Al Maktoum">Sheikh Rashid</a>'s legal advisor, as Secretary General and Legal Advisor to the Council. The council was terminated once the United Arab Emirates was formed.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The tribal nature of society and the lack of definition of borders between emirates frequently led to disputes, settled either through mediation or, more rarely, force. The <a href="/wiki/Trucial_Oman_Scouts" title="Trucial Oman Scouts">Trucial Oman Scouts</a> was a small military force used by the British to keep the peace.
</p><p>In 1953, a subsidiary of BP, D'Arcy Exploration Ltd, obtained an offshore concession from the ruler of Abu Dhabi. BP joined with <a href="/wiki/Compagnie_Fran%C3%A7aise_des_P%C3%A9troles" class="mw-redirect" title="Compagnie Française des Pétroles">Compagnie Française des Pétroles</a> (later <a href="/wiki/Total_S.A." title="Total S.A.">Total</a>) to form operating companies, Abu Dhabi Marine Areas Ltd (ADMA) and Dubai Marine Areas Ltd (DUMA). A number of undersea oil surveys were carried out, including one led by the famous marine explorer <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Cousteau" title="Jacques Cousteau">Jacques Cousteau</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 1958, a floating platform rig was towed from Hamburg, Germany, and positioned over the Umm Shaif pearl bed, in Abu Dhabi waters, where drilling began. In March, it struck oil in the Upper Thamama, a rock formation that would provide many valuable oil finds. This was the first commercial discovery of the Trucial Coast, leading to the first exports of oil in 1962. ADMA made further offshore discoveries at Zakum and elsewhere, and other companies made commercial finds such as the Fateh oilfield off Dubai and the Mubarak field off Sharjah (shared with Iran).<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Meanwhile, onshore exploration was hindered by territorial disputes. In 1955, the United Kingdom represented Abu Dhabi and Oman in their dispute with Saudi Arabia over the <a href="/wiki/Al_Buraimi_Governorate" title="Al Buraimi Governorate">Buraimi Oasis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> A 1974 agreement between Abu Dhabi and Saudi Arabia seemed to have settled the Abu Dhabi-Saudi <a href="/wiki/Territorial_dispute" title="Territorial dispute">border dispute</a>, but this has not been ratified.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The UAE's border with Oman was ratified in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>PDTC continued its onshore exploration away from the disputed area, drilling five more bore holes that were also dry. However, on 27 October 1960, the company discovered oil in commercial quantities at the Murban No. 3 well on the coast near Tarif.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In 1962, PDTC became the Abu Dhabi Petroleum Company. As oil revenues increased, the ruler of Abu Dhabi, <a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Sultan_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan">Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan</a>, undertook a massive construction program, building schools, housing, hospitals and roads. When Dubai's oil exports commenced in 1969, Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Rashid_bin_Saeed_Al_Maktoum" title="Rashid bin Saeed Al Maktoum">Rashid bin Saeed Al Maktoum</a>, the ruler of Dubai, was able to invest the revenues from the limited reserves found to spark the diversification drive that would create the modern <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">global city</a> of Dubai.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence">Independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg/220px-Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg/330px-Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg/440px-Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1023" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag-hoisting_at_the_Union_Declaration.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historic photo depicting the first hoisting of the United Arab Emirates flag by the rulers of the emirates at The Union House, Dubai on 2 December 1971</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg/170px-UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg/255px-UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg/340px-UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="394" data-file-height="589" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UAE_Father_of_the_Nation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Sultan_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan">Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan</a> was the first President of the United Arab Emirates and is recognised as the father of the nation.</div></div></div>
<p>By 1966, it had become clear the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Government of the United Kingdom">British government</a> could no longer afford to administer and protect what is now the United Arab Emirates. British <a href="/wiki/Members_of_Parliament_(UK)" class="mw-redirect" title="Members of Parliament (UK)">MPs</a> debated the preparedness of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> to defend the sheikhdoms. <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Defence" title="Secretary of State for Defence">Secretary of State for Defence</a> <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Denis Healey</a> reported that the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Armed Forces</a> were seriously overstretched and in some respects dangerously under-equipped to defend the sheikhdoms. On 24 January 1968, British Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> announced the government's decision, reaffirmed in March 1971 by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a>, to end the treaty relationships with the seven Trucial Sheikhdoms, that had been, together with Bahrain and Qatar, under British protection. Days after the announcement, the ruler of Abu Dhabi, Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Sultan_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan">Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan</a>, fearing vulnerability, tried to persuade the British to honour the protection treaties by offering to pay the full costs of keeping the <a href="/wiki/British_Armed_Forces" title="British Armed Forces">British Armed Forces</a> in the Emirates. The British <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> government rejected the offer.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> After Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Goronwy_Roberts,_Baron_Goronwy-Roberts" title="Goronwy Roberts, Baron Goronwy-Roberts">Goronwy Roberts</a> informed Sheikh Zayed of the news of British withdrawal, the nine Persian Gulf sheikhdoms attempted to form a union of Arab emirates, but by mid-1971 they were still unable to agree on terms of union even though the British treaty relationship was to expire in December of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Fears of vulnerability were realized the day before independence. An Iranian destroyer group broke formation from an exercise in the lower Gulf, sailing to the <a href="/wiki/Tunb_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Tunb islands">Tunb islands</a>. The islands were taken by force, civilians and Arab defenders alike allowed to flee. A British warship stood idle during the course of the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> A destroyer group approached the island <a href="/wiki/Abu_Musa" title="Abu Musa">Abu Musa</a> as well. But there, Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Khalid_bin_Mohammed_Al_Qasimi" title="Khalid bin Mohammed Al Qasimi">Khalid bin Mohammed Al Qasimi</a>  had already negotiated with the Iranian Shah, and the island was quickly leased to Iran for $3 million a year. Meanwhile, Saudi Arabia laid claim to swathes of Abu Dhabi.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Originally intended to be part of the proposed Federation of Arab Emirates, Bahrain became independent in August, and Qatar in September 1971. When the British-Trucial Sheikhdoms treaty expired on 1 December 1971, they became fully independent.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> On 2 December 1971, at the Dubai Guesthouse, now known as Union House, six of the emirates agreed to enter into a union called the United Arab Emirates.  Ras al-Khaimah joined later, on 10 January 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In February 1972, the Federal National Council (FNC) was created; it was a 40-member consultative body appointed by the seven rulers. The UAE joined the Arab League on 6 December 1971 and the United Nations on 9 December.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> It was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Cooperation_Council_for_the_Arab_States_of_the_Gulf" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooperation Council for the Arab States of the Gulf">Gulf Cooperation Council</a> in May 1981, with Abu Dhabi hosting the first GCC summit.
</p><p>A 19 year old Emirati boy from Abu Dhabi, Abdullah Mohammed Al Maainah, designed the UAE flag in 1971. The main theme of four colors of flag was unity of Arabs nations. The <a href="/wiki/Pan-Arab_colors" title="Pan-Arab colors">Pan-Arab colors</a> red, green, white, and black. It was adopted on 2 December 1971. Al Maainah previously served as the UAE ambassador to Chile and currently serves as the UAE ambassador to the Czech Republic.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Independence_period">Post-Independence period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Post-Independence period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg/220px-Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg/330px-Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg/440px-Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dubai_skyline_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a></div></div></div>
<p>The UAE supported <a href="/wiki/Military_operation" title="Military operation">military operations</a> from the US and other <a href="/wiki/Coalition" title="Coalition">coalition</a> nations engaged in the war against the Taliban in Afghanistan (2001) and Saddam Hussein in Iraq (2003) as well as operations supporting the Global <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> at <a href="/wiki/Al_Dhafra_Air_Base" title="Al Dhafra Air Base">Al Dhafra Air Base</a> located outside of Abu Dhabi. The air base also supported Allied operations during the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">1991 Persian Gulf War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Operation_Northern_Watch" title="Operation Northern Watch">Operation Northern Watch</a>. The country had already signed a <a href="/wiki/Defense_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense (military)">military defense</a> agreement with the U.S. in 1994 and one with France in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> In January 2008, France and the UAE signed a deal allowing France to set up a permanent military base in the emirate of Abu Dhabi.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The UAE joined international military operations in Libya in March 2011.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg/220px-Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="140" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg/330px-Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg/440px-Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5920" data-file-height="3776" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharjah_city_skyline_in_2015.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sharjah city skyline</div></div></div>
<p>On 2 November 2004, the UAE's first president, <a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Sultan_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan">Sheikh Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan</a>, died. His eldest son, Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Khalifa_bin_Zayed_Al_Nahyan" title="Khalifa bin Zayed Al Nahyan">Khalifa bin Zayed Al Nahyan</a>, succeeded as Emir of <a href="/wiki/Abu_Dhabi" title="Abu Dhabi">Abu Dhabi</a>. In accordance with the constitution, the UAE's Supreme Council of Rulers elected Khalifa as president. Sheikh <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Zayed_Al_Nahyan" title="Mohammed bin Zayed Al Nahyan">Mohammed bin Zayed Al Nahyan</a> succeeded Khalifa as Crown Prince of Abu Dhabi.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In January 2006, <a href="/wiki/Maktoum_bin_Rashid_Al_Maktoum" title="Maktoum bin Rashid Al Maktoum">Sheikh Maktoum bin Rashid Al Maktoum</a>, the prime minister of the UAE and the ruler of Dubai, died, and the crown prince <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_bin_Rashid_Al_Maktoum" title="Mohammed bin Rashid Al Maktoum">Sheikh Mohammed bin Rashid Al Maktoum</a> assumed both roles.
</p><p>The first ever national elections were held in the UAE on 16 December 2006. A small number of hand-picked voters chose half of the members of the Federal National Council, an advisory body. The UAE has largely escaped the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a>, which other countries have experienced; however, more than 100 Emirati activists were jailed and <a href="/wiki/Tortured" class="mw-redirect" title="Tortured">tortured</a> because they sought reforms. Furthermore, some people have had their nationality revoked.<sup id="cite_ref-amnesty.org_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-amnesty.org-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Mindful of the protests in nearby Bahrain, in November 2012 the UAE outlawed online mockery of its own government or attempts to organise public protests through social media.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=United_Arab_Emirates&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>.
